//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom_%28ribbon%29.png/44px-Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom_%28ribbon%29.png 2x" data-file-width="218" data-file-height="60" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> (posthumous)</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p><b>Grace Brewster Murray Hopper</b> (<a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">née</a>&#160;<b>Murray</b>; December 9, 1906 – January 1, 1992) was an American <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Rear_admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear admiral (United States)">rear admiral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> One of the first programmers of the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Mark_I" title="Harvard Mark I">Harvard Mark I</a> computer, she was a pioneer of computer programming who invented one of the first <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> related tools. She popularized the idea of machine-independent programming languages, which led to the development of <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a>, an early <a href="/wiki/High-level_programming_language" title="High-level programming language">high-level programming language</a> still in use today.</p>/n<p>Hopper had attempted to enlist in the Navy during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but she was rejected by the military because she was 34 years of age and too old to enlist. She instead joined the Navy Reserves. Hopper began her computing career when she worked on the Harvard Mark I team that was led by <a href="/wiki/Howard_H._Aiken" title="Howard H. Aiken">Howard H. Aiken</a>. In 1949, she joined the <a href="/wiki/Eckert%E2%80%93Mauchly_Computer_Corporation" title="Eckert–Mauchly Computer Corporation">Eckert–Mauchly Computer Corporation</a> and was part of the development team that designed the <a href="/wiki/UNIVAC_I" title="UNIVAC I">UNIVAC I</a> computer in 1944. It was at Eckert–Mauchly that she began developing the compiler. She believed that computer code could be written in English by using a programming language that was based on English words. The compiler would convert that code into <a href="/wiki/Machine_code" title="Machine code">machine code</a> that would be understood by computers. By 1952, Hopper finished her compiler, which was written for the <a href="/wiki/A-0_System" title="A-0 System">A-0 System</a> programming language.<sup id="cite_ref-Spencer85_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer85-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Laplante01_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Laplante01-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bunch93_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bunch93-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Booss03_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booss03-5">[5]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1954, Eckert–Mauchly chose Hopper to lead their department for automatic programming, and she led the release of some of the first compiled languages like <a href="/wiki/FLOW-MATIC" title="FLOW-MATIC">FLOW-MATIC</a>. In 1959, she participated in the <a href="/wiki/CODASYL" title="CODASYL">CODASYL</a> consortium, which consulted Hopper to guide them in creating a machine-independent programming language. This led to the <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a> language, which was inspired by her idea of a language being based on English words. In 1966, she retired from the Naval Reserve, but in 1967, the Navy recalled her to active duty. She retired from the Navy in 1986 and found work as a consultant for the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">Digital Equipment Corporation</a>, sharing her computing experiences.</p>/n<p>Owing to her accomplishments and her naval rank, she was sometimes referred to as "Amazing Grace".<sup id="cite_ref-urlCyber_Heroes_of_the_past:_Amazing_Grace_Hopper_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlCyber_Heroes_of_the_past:_Amazing_Grace_Hopper-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-urlGrace_Murray_Hopper_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlGrace_Murray_Hopper-7">[7]</a></sup> The U.S. Navy <a href="/wiki/Arleigh_Burke-class_destroyer" title="Arleigh Burke-class destroyer"><i>Arleigh Burke</i>-class</a> guided-missile destroyer <a href="/wiki/USS_Hopper" title="USS Hopper">USS&#160;<i>Hopper</i></a> was named for her, as was the <a href="/wiki/Cray_XE6" title="Cray XE6">Cray XE6</a> "Hopper" supercomputer at <a href="/wiki/NERSC" class="mw-redirect" title="NERSC">NERSC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> During her lifetime, Hopper was awarded 40 honorary degrees from universities across the world. A <a href="/wiki/Hopper_College" title="Hopper College">college</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> is named in her honor. In 1991, she received the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Technology_and_Innovation" title="National Medal of Technology and Innovation">National Medal of Technology</a>. On November 22, 2016, she was posthumously awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> by President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div class="toctitle">/n<h2>Contents</h2>/n</div>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life_and_education"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life and education</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Career"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Career</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a <span class="toctext">UNIVAC</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#COBOL"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">COBOL</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Standards"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Standards</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Retirement"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Retirement</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Post_retirement"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Post retirement</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Dates_of_rank"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Dates of rank</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Awards_and_honors"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Awards and honors</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Military_awards"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Military awards</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Other_awards"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Other awards</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Anecdotes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Anecdotes</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Obituary_notices"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Obituary notices</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">/n<tr>/n<td class="mbox-image">/n<div class="center">/n<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Audio-input-microphone.svg/50px-Audio-input-microphone.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Audio-input-microphone.svg/75px-Audio-input-microphone.svg.png 1.5x, class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">/n<div class="haudio">/n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper_(As_Told_By_U.S._Chief_Technology_Officer_Megan_Smith).oggvorbis.ogg" title="File:Grace Hopper (As Told By U.S. Chief Technology Officer Megan Smith).oggvorbis.ogg">Grace Hopper (As Told By U.S. Chief Technology Officer Megan Smith)</a></div>/n<div>/n<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-dir="ltr" /></audio></div>/n</div>/n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div>/n</div>/n</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em">/n<hr />/n<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p>Hopper was born in New York City. She was the eldest of three children. Her parents, Walter Fletcher Murray and Mary Campbell Van Horne, were of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> descent, and attended <a href="/wiki/West_End_Collegiate_Church" title="West End Collegiate Church">West End Collegiate Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Her great-grandfather, Alexander Wilson Russell, an admiral in the US Navy, fought in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mobile_Bay" title="Battle of Mobile Bay">Battle of Mobile Bay</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.</p>/n<p>Grace was very curious as a child; this was a lifelong trait. At the age of seven, she decided to determine how an alarm clock worked and dismantled seven alarm clocks before her mother realized what she was doing (she was then limited to one clock).<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> For her <a href="/wiki/University-preparatory_school" title="University-preparatory school">preparatory school</a> education, she attended the <a href="/wiki/Wardlaw-Hartridge_School" title="Wardlaw-Hartridge School">Hartridge School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Plainfield,_New_Jersey" title="Plainfield, New Jersey">Plainfield, New Jersey</a>. Hopper was initially rejected for early admission to <a href="/wiki/Vassar_College" title="Vassar College">Vassar College</a> at age 16 (her test scores in Latin were too low), but she was admitted the following year. She graduated <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa_Society" title="Phi Beta Kappa Society">Phi Beta Kappa</a> from Vassar in 1928 with a bachelor's degree in mathematics and physics and earned her master's degree at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> in 1930.</p>/n<p>In 1934, she earned a Ph.D. in mathematics from Yale<sup id="cite_ref-NWHM_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NWHM-12">[12]</a></sup> under the direction of <a href="/wiki/%C3%98ystein_Ore" title="Øystein Ore">Øystein Ore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenladuke09_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenladuke09-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Her <a href="/wiki/Dissertation" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissertation">dissertation</a>, <i>New Types of Irreducibility Criteria</i>, was published that same year.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Hopper began teaching mathematics at Vassar in 1931, and was promoted to associate professor in 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogilvie_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogilvie-16">[16]</a></sup></p>/n<p>She was married to <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> professor Vincent Foster Hopper (1906–76) from 1930 until their divorce in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-greenladuke09_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenladuke09-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> She did not marry again, but chose to retain his surname.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg/220px-Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg/330px-Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg/440px-Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Mark_I_sign-up.agr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nHopper's signatures on a duty officer signup sheet for the Bureau of Ships Computation Project at Harvard, which built and operated the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Mark_I" title="Harvard Mark I">Mark I</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Hopper had tried to enlist in the Navy early in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. She was rejected for multiple reasons. At age 34, she was too old to enlist, and her weight to height ratio was too low. She was also denied on the basis that her job as a mathematician and mathematics professor at Vassar College was valuable to the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> During the war in 1943, Hopper obtained a leave of absence from Vassar and was sworn into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Reserve" title="United States Navy Reserve">United States Navy Reserve</a>; she was one of many women who volunteered to serve in the <a href="/wiki/WAVES" title="WAVES">WAVES</a>. She had to get an exemption to enlist; she was 15 pounds (6.8&#160;kg) below the Navy minimum weight of 120 pounds (54&#160;kg). She reported in December and trained at the Naval Reserve Midshipmen's School at <a href="/wiki/Smith_College" title="Smith College">Smith College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton, Massachusetts</a>. Hopper graduated first in her class in 1944, and was assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Ships" title="Bureau of Ships">Bureau of Ships</a> Computation Project at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> as a lieutenant, junior grade. She served on the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Mark_I" title="Harvard Mark I">Mark I computer</a> programming staff headed by <a href="/wiki/Howard_H._Aiken" title="Howard H. Aiken">Howard H. Aiken</a>. Hopper and Aiken co-authored three papers on the Mark I, also known as the Automatic Sequence Controlled Calculator. Hopper's request to transfer to the regular Navy at the end of the war was declined due to her advanced age of 38. She continued to serve in the Navy Reserve. Hopper remained at the Harvard Computation Lab until 1949, turning down a full professorship at Vassar in favor of working as a research fellow under a Navy contract at Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-KBW_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KBW-19">[19]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tleft">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Murray_Hopper,_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC,_1978,_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg/220px-Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg/330px-Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg/440px-Grace_Murray_Hopper%2C_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC%2C_1978%2C_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="782" data-file-height="1200" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Murray_Hopper,_in_her_office_in_Washington_DC,_1978,_%C2%A9Lynn_Gilbert.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nHopper in a computer room in Washington DC, 1978, photographed by <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Gilbert" title="Lynn Gilbert">Lynn Gilbert</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UNIVAC">UNIVAC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: UNIVAC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In 1949, Hopper became an employee of the <a href="/wiki/Eckert%E2%80%93Mauchly_Computer_Corporation" title="Eckert–Mauchly Computer Corporation">Eckert–Mauchly Computer Corporation</a> as a senior mathematician and joined the team developing the <a href="/wiki/UNIVAC_I" title="UNIVAC I">UNIVAC I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogilvie_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogilvie-16">[16]</a></sup> Hopper also served as UNIVAC director of Automatic Programming Development for Remington Rand. The UNIVAC was the first known large-scale electronic computer to be on the market in 1950, and was more competitive at processing information than the Mark I.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>/n<p>When Hopper recommended the development a new programming language that would use entirely English words, she "was told very quickly that [she] couldn't do this because computers didn't understand English." Her idea was not accepted for 3 years, and she published her first paper on the subject, compilers, in 1952. In the early 1950s, the company was taken over by the <a href="/wiki/Remington_Rand" title="Remington Rand">Remington Rand</a> corporation, and it was while she was working for them that her original <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> work was done. The program was known as the A compiler and its first version was <a href="/wiki/A-0_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="A-0 programming language">A-0</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mcgee2004_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgee2004-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup></p>/n<p>In 1952 she had an operational link-loader, which at the time was referred to as a compiler. She later said that "Nobody believed that," and that she "had a running compiler and nobody would touch it. They told me computers could only do arithmetic."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> She goes on to say that her compiler "translated mathematical notation into machine code. Manipulating symbols was fine for mathematicians but it was no good for data processors who were not symbol manipulators. Very few people are really symbol manipulators. If they are they become professional mathematicians, not data processors. It’s much easier for most people to write an English statement than it is to use symbols. So I decided data processors ought to be able to write their programs in English, and the computers would translate them into machine code. That was the beginning of COBOL, a computer language for data processors. I could say “Subtract income tax from pay” instead of trying to write that in octal code or using all kinds of symbols. COBOL is the major language used today in data processing.”<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>“</p>/n<p>In 1954 Hopper was named the company's first director of automatic programming, and her department released some of the first compiler-based programming languages, including <a href="/wiki/MATH-MATIC" title="MATH-MATIC">MATH-MATIC</a> and <a href="/wiki/FLOW-MATIC" title="FLOW-MATIC">FLOW-MATIC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogilvie_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogilvie-16">[16]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL">COBOL</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: COBOL">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg/220px-Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg/330px-Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg/440px-Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="513" data-file-height="450" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper_and_UNIVAC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nHopper at the <a href="/wiki/UNIVAC_I" title="UNIVAC I">UNIVAC I</a> console, c. 1960</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In the spring of 1959, computer experts from industry and government were brought together in a two-day conference known as the Conference on Data Systems Languages (<a href="/wiki/CODASYL" title="CODASYL">CODASYL</a>). Hopper served as a technical consultant to the committee, and many of her former employees served on the short-term committee that defined the new language <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a> (an acronym for <b>CO</b>mmon <b>B</b>usiness-<b>O</b>riented <b>L</b>anguage). The new language extended Hopper's FLOW-MATIC language with some ideas from the <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> equivalent, <a href="/wiki/COMTRAN" title="COMTRAN">COMTRAN</a>. Hopper's belief that programs should be written in a language that was close to English (rather than in <a href="/wiki/Machine_code" title="Machine code">machine code</a> or in languages close to machine code, such as <a href="/wiki/Assembly_language" title="Assembly language">assembly languages</a>) was captured in the new business language, and COBOL went on to be the most ubiquitous business language to date.<sup id="cite_ref-KWB_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KWB-24">[24]</a></sup> Among the members of the committee that worked on COBOL was <a href="/wiki/Mount_Holyoke_College" title="Mount Holyoke College">Mount Holyoke College</a> alumni <a href="/wiki/Jean_E._Sammet" title="Jean E. Sammet">Jean E. Sammet</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>/n<p>From 1967 to 1977, Hopper served as the director of the Navy Programming Languages Group in the Navy's Office of Information Systems Planning and was promoted to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_O-6)" title="Captain (United States O-6)">captain</a> in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-KBW_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KBW-19">[19]</a></sup> She developed validation software for COBOL and its compiler as part of a COBOL standardization program for the entire Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-KBW_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KBW-19">[19]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards">Standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In the 1970s, Hopper advocated for the Defense Department to replace large, centralized systems with networks of small, distributed computers. Any user on any computer node could access common databases located on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-mcgee2004_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgee2004-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:119</sup> She developed the implementation of <a href="/wiki/Standardization" title="Standardization">standards</a> for testing computer systems and components, most significantly for early <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/FORTRAN" class="mw-redirect" title="FORTRAN">FORTRAN</a> and COBOL. The Navy tests for conformance to these standards led to significant convergence among the programming language dialects of the major computer vendors. In the 1980s, these tests (and their official administration) were assumed by the National Bureau of Standards (NBS), known today as the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST).</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tleft">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG/170px-Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG" width="170" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG/255px-Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG/340px-Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2389" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper_being_promoted_to_Commodore.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nHopper being promoted to the rank of commodore in 1983</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In accordance with Navy attrition regulations, Hopper retired from the Naval Reserve with the rank of <a href="/wiki/Commander_(United_States)" title="Commander (United States)">commander</a> at age 60 at the end of 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-urlAttrition.2FRetirement_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlAttrition.2FRetirement-26">[26]</a></sup> She was recalled to active duty in August 1967 for a six-month period that turned into an indefinite assignment. She again retired in 1971 but was again asked to return to active duty in 1972. She was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(U.S._Navy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Captain (U.S. Navy)">captain</a> in 1973 by <a href="/wiki/Admiral_(United_States)" title="Admiral (United States)">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Elmo_R._Zumwalt,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Elmo R. Zumwalt, Jr.">Elmo R. Zumwalt, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-navybio_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navybio-27">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<p>After <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> Representative <a href="/wiki/Philip_Crane" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Crane">Philip Crane</a> saw her on a March 1983 segment of <i><a href="/wiki/60_Minutes" title="60 Minutes">60 Minutes</a></i>, he championed <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.congress.gov/bill/98th-congress/house-joint-resolution/341">H.J.Res. 341</a>, a joint <a href="/wiki/Resolution_(law)" title="Resolution (law)">resolution</a> originating in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, which led to her promotion to <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(United_States)" title="Commodore (United States)">commodore</a> by special Presidential appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-navybio_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navybio-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DavidLetterman86_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidLetterman86-30">[30]</a></sup> She remained on active duty for several years beyond mandatory retirement by special approval of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Effective November 8, 1985, the rank of commodore was renamed <a href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear Admiral (United States)">rear admiral</a> (lower half) and Hopper became one of the Navy's few female admirals.</p>/n<p>Following a career that spanned more than 42 years, Admiral Hopper took mandatory retirement from the Navy on August 14, 1986. At a celebration held in Boston on the <a href="/wiki/USS_Constitution" title="USS Constitution">USS&#160;<i>Constitution</i></a> to commemorate her retirement, Hopper was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Defense_Distinguished_Service_Medal" title="Defense Distinguished Service Medal">Defense Distinguished Service Medal</a>, the highest non-combat decoration awarded by the Department of Defense.</p>/n<p>At the time of her retirement, she was the oldest active-duty commissioned officer in the United States Navy (79 years, eight months and five days), and aboard the oldest commissioned ship in the United States Navy (188 years, nine months and 23 days).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> (Admirals <a href="/wiki/William_D._Leahy" title="William D. Leahy">William D. Leahy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chester_W._Nimitz" title="Chester W. Nimitz">Chester W. Nimitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyman_G._Rickover" title="Hyman G. Rickover">Hyman G. Rickover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_(1778%E2%80%931869)" title="Charles Stewart (1778–1869)">Charles Stewart</a> were the only other officers in the Navy's history to serve on active duty at a higher age. Leahy and Nimitz served on active duty for life due to their promotions to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Admiral_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fleet Admiral (United States)">fleet admiral</a>.)</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_retirement">Post retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Post retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p>Following her retirement from the Navy, she was hired as a senior consultant to <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> (DEC). Hopper was initially offered the job position by Rita Yavinsky, but she insisted on applying for the position at <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">Digital Equipment Corporation</a>, and going through the typical formal interview process. She also sent a letter to Yavinsky's boss explaining that she would be available on alternating Thursdays, receiving a high salary, and have access to an unlimited expense account if she were to be exhibited at their museum of computing as a pioneer. After the proposal from Hopper, she was hired as a full-time senior consultant. As part of her position, she would report to Yavinsky. In this position, Hopper represented the company at industry forums, serving on various industry committees, along with other obligations.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> She retained that position until her death at age 85 in 1992.</p>/n<p>Hopper was a goodwill ambassador in her primary activity in this capacity. She lectured widely about the early days of computing, her career, and on efforts that computer vendors could take to make life easier for their users. She visited most of Digital's engineering facilities, where she generally received a standing ovation at the conclusion of her remarks.</p>/n<p>She often recounted that during her service she was frequently asked by admirals and generals why satellite communication would take so long. So during many of her lectures, she illustrated a nanosecond using salvaged obsolete Bell System 25 pair telephone cable, cut it to 11.8&#160; inch (30&#160; cm) lengths, <a href="/wiki/Light-nanosecond" class="mw-redirect" title="Light-nanosecond">the distance that light travels in one nanosecond</a>, and handed out the individual wires to her listeners. Although no longer a serving officer, she always wore her Navy full dress uniform to these lectures, which is allowed by US Navy uniform regulations.</p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>The most important thing I've accomplished, other than building the compiler, is training young people. They come to me, you know, and say, 'Do you think we can do this?' I say, "Try it." And I back 'em up. They need that. I keep track of them as they get older and I stir 'em up at intervals so they don't forget to take chances.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>/n</blockquote>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p>On New Year's Day 1992, Hopper died in her sleep of natural causes at her home in Arlington, Virginia; she was 85 years of age. She was interred with full military honors in <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates_of_rank">Dates of rank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dates of rank">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<ul>/n<li>Ensign - December 1943</li>/n<li>Lieutenant (junior grade) - June 27, 1944</li>/n<li>Lieutenant - January 1, 1946</li>/n<li>Lieutenant Commander - April 1, 1952</li>/n<li>Commander - July 1, 1957</li>/n<li>Retired - December 31, 1966</li>/n<li>Recalled to active duty - August 1967</li>/n<li>Retired - 1971</li>/n<li>Recalled to active duty - 1972</li>/n<li>Captain - August 2, 1973</li>/n<li>Commodore - December 15, 1983</li>/n<li>Rear Admiral (Lower Half) - November 8, 1985</li>/n<li>Final retirement - August 31, 1986</li>/n</ul>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_awards">Military awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span 1973, 1983)</center>/n</td>/n<td colspan="2" align="center">/n<center><a href="/wiki/Naval_Reserve_Medal" title="Naval Reserve Medal">Naval Reserve Medal</a><br />/n(1953)</center>/n</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n</center>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_awards">Other awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<ul>/n<li>1964: Hopper was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Women_Engineers" title="Society of Women Engineers">Society of Women Engineers</a> Achievement Award, the Society’s highest honor, “In recognition of her significant contributions to the burgeoning computer industry as an engineering manager and originator of automatic programming systems.”.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1969: Hopper was awarded the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Information_Technology_Professionals" title="Association of Information Technology Professionals">Data Processing Management Association</a> Man of the Year award (now called the Distinguished Information Sciences Award).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1971: The annual <a href="/wiki/Grace_Murray_Hopper_Award" title="Grace Murray Hopper Award">Grace Murray Hopper Award for Outstanding Young Computer Professionals</a> was established in 1971 by the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">Association for Computing Machinery</a>.</li>/n<li>1973: First American and the first woman of any nationality to be made a <a href="/wiki/DFBCS" class="mw-redirect" title="DFBCS">Distinguished Fellow of the British Computer Society</a>.</li>/n<li>1982: <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_University_Women" title="American Association of University Women">American Association of University Women</a> Achievement Award and an Honorary Doctor of Science from <a href="/wiki/Marquette_University" title="Marquette University">Marquette University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1985: Honorary Doctor of Letters from <a href="/wiki/Western_New_England_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Western New England College">Western New England College</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Western_New_England_University" title="Western New England University">Western New England University</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1986: Upon her retirement, she received the Defense Distinguished Service Medal.</li>/n<li>1987: The first <a href="/wiki/Computer_History_Museum" title="Computer History Museum">Computer History Museum</a> Fellow Award Recipient "for contributions to the development of programming languages, for standardization efforts, and for lifelong naval service."<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1988: Golden Gavel Award at the <a href="/wiki/Toastmasters_International" title="Toastmasters International">Toastmasters International</a> convention in Washington, DC.</li>/n<li>1991: <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="National Medal of Technology">National Medal of Technology</a>.</li>/n<li>1991: Elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-42">[42]</a></sup></li>/n<li>1996: <a href="/wiki/USS_Hopper_(DDG-70)" class="mw-redirect" title="USS Hopper (DDG-70)">USS&#160;<i>Hopper</i>&#160;(DDG-70)</a> was launched. Nicknamed <i>Amazing Grace</i>, it is on a very short <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._military_vessels_named_after_women" title="List of U.S. military vessels named after women">list of U.S. military vessels named after women</a>.</li>/n<li>2001: <a href="/wiki/Eavan_Boland" title="Eavan Boland">Eavan Boland</a> wrote a poem dedicated to Grace Hopper titled "Code" in her 2001 release <i>Against Love Poetry</i>.</li>/n<li>2001: The Gracies, the Government Technology Leadership Award were named in her honor.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>/n<li>2009: The Department of Energy's <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Research_Scientific_Computing_Center" title="National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center">National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center</a> named its flagship system "Hopper".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>/n<li>2009: <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Intelligence" title="Office of Naval Intelligence">Office of Naval Intelligence</a> creates the Grace Hopper Information Services Center.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>/n<li>2013: Google made the <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a> for Hopper's 107th birthday an animation of her sitting at a computer, using COBOL to print out her age. At the end of the animation, a moth flies out of the computer.<sup id="cite_ref-Google_Doodle_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Doodle-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>/n<li>2016: On November 22, 2016 Hopper was posthumously awarded a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> for her accomplishments in the field of computer science.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>/n<li>2017: <a href="/wiki/Hopper_College" title="Hopper College">Hopper College</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> was named in her honor.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<ul>/n<li>On February 11, 2017 <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> announced its intent to rename <a href="/wiki/Calhoun_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Calhoun College">Calhoun College</a>, one of its twelve undergraduate residential colleges, after Hopper following years of controversy about its previous namesake <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>. Hopper was a graduate of Yale University, receiving an M.A. in 1930 and a Ph.D in 1934.</li>/n<li><b>The Grace Hopper Celebration of Women in Computing</b> is a convention for Women in the field of Computer Science and Technology. It is named after Hopper to honor her for her work and influence in the field of computing, and her push for more women to enter and stay in the tech field. It features a wide array of educational and professional development courses and workshops, including a lesson on compilers, which Hopper invented and pioneered, and a career fair, in order to help connect women in the computing field with potential employers.</li>/n<li>The Navy's <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Numerical_Meteorology_and_Oceanography_Center" title="Fleet Numerical Meteorology and Oceanography Center">Fleet Numerical Meteorology and Oceanography Center</a> is located at 7 Grace Hopper Avenue in <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>; the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a>'s San Francisco / Monterey Bay Area Hydrology / Geomorphology office is at 21 Grace Hopper Avenue.</li>/n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_M._Hopper_Navy_Regional_Data_Automation_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Grace M. Hopper Navy Regional Data Automation Center (page does not exist)">Grace M. Hopper Navy Regional Data Automation Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station,_North_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval Air Station, North Island">Naval Air Station, North Island</a>, California.</li>/n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Grace_Murray_Hopper_Park" title="Grace Murray Hopper Park">Grace Murray Hopper Park</a></i>, located on South Joyce Street in <a href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arlington, Virginia">Arlington, Virginia</a>, is a small memorial park in front of her former residence (River House Apartments) and is now owned by <a href="/wiki/Arlington_County,_Virginia" title="Arlington County, Virginia">Arlington County, Virginia</a>.</li>/n<li>Women at <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Corporation">Microsoft Corporation</a> formed an employee group called Hoppers and established a scholarship in her honor. Hoppers has over 3000 members worldwide.</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Brewster_Academy" title="Brewster Academy">Brewster Academy</a>, a school located in <a href="/wiki/Wolfeboro,_New_Hampshire" title="Wolfeboro, New Hampshire">Wolfeboro, New Hampshire</a>, United States, dedicated their computer lab to her in 1985, calling it the Grace Murray Hopper Center for Computer Learning.<sup id="cite_ref-navybio_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navybio-27">[27]</a></sup> The academy bestows a Grace Murray Hopper Prize to a graduate who excelled in the field of computer systems.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Hopper had spent her childhood summers at a family home in Wolfeboro.</li>/n<li>An administration building on Naval Support Activity Annapolis (previously known as Naval Station Annapolis) in Annapolis, Maryland is named the Grace Hopper Building in her honor.<sup id="cite_ref-navybio_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navybio-27">[27]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Walter_E._Carter_Jr." title="Walter E. Carter Jr.">Vice Admiral Walter E. "Ted" Carter</a> announced on 8 September 2016 at the Athena Conference that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">Naval Academy</a>'s newest Cyber Operations building would be named Hopper Hall after Admiral Grace Hopper. This is the first building at any service academy named after a woman. In his words, "Grace Hopper was the admiral of the Cyber Seas."</li>/n<li>The US Naval Academy also owns a Cray XC-30 supercomputer named "Grace," hosted at the University of Maryland-College Park.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Building 1482 aboard <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_North_Island" title="Naval Air Station North Island">Naval Air Station North Island</a>, housing the Naval Computer and Telecommunication Station San Diego, is named the Grace Hopper Building.</li>/n<li>Building 6007, C2/CNT West, Command, control, communications, computers, intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance, or <a href="/wiki/C4ISTAR" class="mw-redirect" title="C4ISTAR">C4ISR</a>, Center of Excellence in <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Proving_Ground" title="Aberdeen Proving Ground">Aberdeen Proving Ground</a>, Maryland is named the Rear Admiral Grace Hopper Building.</li>/n<li>A named professorship in the Department of Computer Sciences was established at Yale University in her honor. <a href="/wiki/Joan_Feigenbaum" title="Joan Feigenbaum">Joan Feigenbaum</a> was named to this chair in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Grace Hopper's legacy was an inspiring factor in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Grace_Hopper_Celebration_of_Women_in_Computing" title="Grace Hopper Celebration of Women in Computing">Grace Hopper Celebration of Women in Computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Held yearly, this conference is designed to bring the research and career interests of women in computing to the forefront.</li>/n<li>Grace Hopper Academy is a for-profit immersive programming school in New York City named in Grace Hopper's honor. It opened in January 2016 with the goal of increasing the proportion of women in software engineering careers.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>/n<li>A bridge over Goose Creek, to join the north and south sides of the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Support_Activity_Charleston" title="Naval Support Activity Charleston">Naval Support Activity Charleston</a> side of <a href="/wiki/Joint_Base_Charleston" title="Joint Base Charleston">Joint Base Charleston</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, is named the Grace Hopper Memorial Bridge in her honor.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Grace Hopper was awarded 40 honorary degrees from universities worldwide during her lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Beginning in 2015, one of the nine competition fields at the <a href="/wiki/FIRST_Robotics_Competition" title="FIRST Robotics Competition">FIRST Robotics Competition</a> world championship is named for Hopper.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>/n<li><i>Born with Curiosity: The Grace Hopper Story</i> is an upcoming <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anecdotes">Anecdotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anecdotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:H96566k.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/H96566k.jpg/220px-H96566k.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/H96566k.jpg/330px-H96566k.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/H96566k.jpg/440px-H96566k.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="583" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:H96566k.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nPhoto of "first <a href="/wiki/Software_bug" title="Software bug">computer bug</a>" (a moth)</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Throughout much of her later career, Hopper was much in demand as a speaker at various computer-related events. She was well known for her lively and irreverent speaking style, as well as a rich treasury of early war stories. She also received the nickname "Grandma COBOL".</p>/n<ul>/n<li>While she was working on a <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Mark_II" title="Harvard Mark II">Mark II</a> Computer at a US Navy research lab in Dahlgren, Virginia in 1947, her associates discovered a <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moth</a> that was stuck in a <a href="/wiki/Relay" title="Relay">relay</a>; the moth impeded the operation of the relay. While neither Hopper nor her crew mentioned the phrase "debugging" in their logs, the case was held as an instance of literal "debugging." For many years, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Computer_bug" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer bug">bug</a></i> had been in use in engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The remains of the moth can be found in the group's log book at the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_American_History" title="National Museum of American History">National Museum of American History</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Grace Hopper is famous for her <i>nanoseconds</i> visual aid. People (such as generals and admirals) used to ask her why <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> communication took so long. She started handing out pieces of wire that were just under one foot long (11.80 inches)—the distance that light travels in one <a href="/wiki/Nanosecond" title="Nanosecond">nanosecond</a>. She gave these pieces of wire the <a href="/wiki/Metonymy" title="Metonymy">metonym</a> "nanoseconds."<sup id="cite_ref-DavidLetterman86_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidLetterman86-30">[30]</a></sup> She was careful to tell her audience that the length of her nanoseconds was actually the maximum speed the signals would travel in a vacuum, and that signals would travel more slowly through the actual wires that were her teaching aids. Later she used the same pieces of wire to illustrate why computers had to be small to be fast. At many of her talks and visits, she handed out "nanoseconds" to everyone in the audience, contrasting them with a coil of wire 984 feet long<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>, representing a <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microsecond</a>. Later, while giving these lectures while working for DEC, she passed out packets of pepper, calling the individual grains of ground pepper <a href="/wiki/Picosecond" title="Picosecond">picoseconds</a>.</li>/n</ul>/n<p>Jay Elliot described Grace Hopper as appearing to be "'all Navy', but when you reach inside, you find a 'Pirate' dying to be released".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Obituary_notices">Obituary notices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Grace_Hopper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Obituary notices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<ul>/n<li>Betts, Mitch (<i><a href="/wiki/Computerworld" title="Computerworld">Computerworld</a></i> 26: 14, 1992)</li>/n<li>Bromberg, Howard (<i><a href="/wiki/IEEE_Software" title="IEEE Software">IEEE Software</a></i> 9: 103–104, 1992)</li>/n<li>Danca, Richard A. (<i>Federal Computer Week</i> 6: 26–27, 1992)</li>/n<li>Hancock, Bill (<i>Digital Review</i> 9: 40, 1992)</li>/n<li>Power, Kevin (<i>Government Computer News</i> 11: 70, 1992)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Jean_E._Sammet" title="Jean E. Sammet">Sammet, J. E.</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Communications_of_the_ACM" title="Communications of the ACM">Communications of the ACM</a></i> 35 (4): 128–131, 1992)</li>/n<li>Weiss, Eric A. (<i><a href="/wiki/IEEE_Annals_of_the_History_of_Computing" title="IEEE Annals of the History of Computing">IEEE Annals of the History of Computing</a></i> 14: 56–58, 1992)</li>/n</ul>/n<h2><span
